<script>
  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_949f0976-fee9-415d-931a-04b72b8560c8'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }
  //]]>
</script>

<div id="descriptionContainer">
  
      <div id="description" class="readable stacked" style="right:0">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer7807996620029166966"><b>“One of America’s greatest novelists” dazzlingly reinvents the coming-of-age story in his most passionate and surprising book to date</b><br/><br/>Sinuously constructed in four interlocking parts, Paul Auster’s fifteenth novel opens in New York City in the spring of 1967, when twenty-year-old Adam Walker, an aspiring poet and student at Columbia University, meets the enigmatic Frenchman</span>
  <span id="freeText7807996620029166966" style="display:none"><b>“One of America’s greatest novelists” dazzlingly reinvents the coming-of-age story in his most passionate and surprising book to date</b><br /><br />Sinuously constructed in four interlocking parts, Paul Auster’s fifteenth novel opens in New York City in the spring of 1967, when twenty-year-old Adam Walker, an aspiring poet and student at Columbia University, meets the enigmatic Frenchman Rudolf Born and his silent and seductive girfriend, Margot. Before long, Walker finds himself caught in a perverse triangle that leads to a sudden, shocking act of violence that will alter the course of his life.<br /><br />Three different narrators tell the story of Invisible, a novel that travels in time from 1967 to 2007 and moves from Morningside Heights, to the Left Bank of Paris, to a remote island in the Caribbean. It is a book of youthful rage, unbridled sexual hunger, and a relentless quest for justice. With uncompromising insight, Auster takes us into the shadowy borderland between truth and memory, between authorship and identity, to produce a work of unforgettable power that confirms his reputation as “one of America’s most spectacularly inventive writers.” </span>
  <a data-text-id="7807996620029166966" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    The richness of the book is to vacillate between reality and fantasy. However, the narrative is looking always for the truth: Jim goes finding the peo<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    The richness of the book is to vacillate between reality and fantasy. However, the narrative is looking always for the truth: Jim goes finding the people involved in Paris, looks for the NY police reports related with the murder; at the end, one personage goes to find Born and realize who he really was. So, everything that has been related by Adam is completely true. So to say, indirectly, that also the relationship with the sister would be true… I believe…<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer116942549" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer13837397844915067352"><i>I shook his hand for the first time in the spring of 1967. I was a second-year student at Columbia then, a know-nothing boy with an appetite for books and a belief (or delusion) that one day I would become good enough to call myself a poet,...</i><br/>I love Paul Auster, there's no way around it. I am always intrigued, fascinated and sometimes completely in the dark but he is such a great writer for me. Have read several of his books and fortunately I have some more to go, but this one, again, a gem. Dar</span>
  <span id="freeText13837397844915067352" style="display:none"><i>I shook his hand for the first time in the spring of 1967. I was a second-year student at Columbia then, a know-nothing boy with an appetite for books and a belief (or delusion) that one day I would become good enough to call myself a poet,...</i><br />I love Paul Auster, there's no way around it. I am always intrigued, fascinated and sometimes completely in the dark but he is such a great writer for me. Have read several of his books and fortunately I have some more to go, but this one, again, a gem. Dark, intriguing, what's going on, how is this going to end....beautiful writing... Yes, the end is a bit weird but for me, somehow it fitted perfectly in the storyline. Auster does not give any answers, sometimes it is hard to follow a storyline, but this book was easily readible for me. His stories can be hard to read sometimes, but this one was quite a smooth story as I see it. Three persons in the book tell their part and view of the story. Outstanding. Therefore again, 5 stars. More to follow as usual.<br /><br />This is the story outline: Twenty-year old Adam Walker, an aspiring poet, is studying in New York City when he meets the enigmatic Frenchman Rudolf Born, and his silent and seductive girlfriend Margot. Before long, Walker finds himself caught in a love triangle that leads to a sudden, shocking act of violence that will alter the course of his life....</span>

<span id="freeTextContainer6928529434976923557">Invisible, Paul Auster <br/><br/><b>Invisible</b> is a novel by <b>Paul Auster</b> published in 2009 by Henry Holt and Company. <br/><br/>The book is divided into four parts, telling a continuous story, but each section told in a different voice, and by several different authors. <br/><br/>The first section, titled "Spring" and told in first person. <br/><br/>The second section, "Summer" describes the events in Adam's life later that summer in New York sharing an apartment with his older sister, Gwyn. This section of the story is told in second per</span>
  <span id="freeText6928529434976923557" style="display:none">Invisible, Paul Auster <br /><br /><b>Invisible</b> is a novel by <b>Paul Auster</b> published in 2009 by Henry Holt and Company. <br /><br />The book is divided into four parts, telling a continuous story, but each section told in a different voice, and by several different authors. <br /><br />The first section, titled "Spring" and told in first person. <br /><br />The second section, "Summer" describes the events in Adam's life later that summer in New York sharing an apartment with his older sister, Gwyn. This section of the story is told in second person. <br /><br />In the third section, "Fall" we learn that Adam, in 2007, has died before he and James could meet, and has completed only notes of the third and final section of his memoir of 1967. <br /><br />The final section takes place in 2007. James has been told by Gwyn that the major events of the second section of the book are entirely made-up, and James wonders whether any of the purported memoir is true. ...<br /><br />تاریخ نخستین خوانش: روز دوازدهم ماه دسامبر سال 2009 میلادی<br /><br />عنوان: ناپیدا؛ نویسنده: پل آستر (اوستر)؛ مترجم: خجسته کیهان؛ تهران، افق، 1388؛ در 268ص؛ شابک 9789643696146؛ چاپ دوم 1389؛ چاپ سوم 1391؛ چاپ دیگر 1393؛ موضوع: داستانهای نویسندگان امریکایی - سده 21م<br /><br />ادبیات معمایی، و کاویدن زندگی فردی رو به زوال است؛<br /><br />نقل از متن: «چاقو از ضامنش بیرون آمد، پسر که چاقو شکمش را میدرید، ناله کرد. گفت: چرا میزنی؟ هفت تیرم که گلوله ندارد». پایان نقل از متن<br /><br />کتاب «ناپيدا» نخستین اثر«پل آستر» نویسنده ی رمانهای سه گانه ی نیویوک در سبک «پست مدرن» است، كه نشر افق، با ترجمه ی بانو «خجسته کیهان»، همزمان با انتشار جهانی اثر، آنرا منتشر كرده است؛ نويسنده، در اين رمان نيز، داستانهايی تودرتو، میآفریند، كه گاه به ادبيات معمايی شباهت دارند؛ «آستر» با كاويدن زندگی رو به زوال «آدام واكر»، پردازش شخصيت منفی را به اوج میرساند؛ ا. شربیانی</span>

<div id="review_112883203" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/112883203" />
    <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters"><img alt="Elyse  Walters" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514246949p2/597461.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/112883203?book_show_action=true">Apr 01, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Elyse  Walters" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Elyse  Walters" href="/user/show/597461-elyse-walters">Elyse  Walters</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer112883203" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12416048574796307105">How often does one have the opportunity to walk into a bar, meet a person, for the first time, and then be offered -(all finances covered) - to write a literary magazine? <br/><br/>The year is 1967....<br/>This novel is divided into four sections....<br/>and.....<br/>I LOVE PAUL AUSTER....<br/> another wonderful Auster novel!!! <br/>This time - I ‘read’ (the physical book), but I could almost hear Paul Auster’s voice reading it ... <br/>like the last few audiobooks I had listened to by him. <br/><br/>In section one: [Spring]<br/> Adam Walker, twenty</span>
  <span id="freeText12416048574796307105" style="display:none">How often does one have the opportunity to walk into a bar, meet a person, for the first time, and then be offered -(all finances covered) - to write a literary magazine? <br /><br />The year is 1967....<br />This novel is divided into four sections....<br />and.....<br />I LOVE PAUL AUSTER....<br /> another wonderful Auster novel!!! <br />This time - I ‘read’ (the physical book), but I could almost hear Paul Auster’s voice reading it ... <br />like the last few audiobooks I had listened to by him. <br /><br />In section one: [Spring]<br /> Adam Walker, twenty years old, was an aspiring poet - an undergraduate at Columbia University. He meets the callabistic/ persuasive tutor.....Frenchman Rudolf Born.... political science professor, and his girlfriend Margot. Margot liked the young poet. She had good taste: me too!!!<br /><br />I was intrigued by all the characters and the I loved the vibrancy - mood - and tone of the entire novel - <br />New York, Paris, ... even Oakland, California. <br /><br />A love triangle develops ( not as steamy as your naughty mind thinks though), leading to repercussions of violence. ( hopefully you can’t imagine this - until you read it yourself)....😏<br /><br />In section two: [Summer]<br />Adam was sharing an apartment with his sister Gwen....and was not well. He was slowly dying of leukemia. He sent his manuscript to a guy named Jim, a well known author. Jim agrees to meet with Adam. <br /><br />In section three: [fall]<br />Adam died before he met with Jim in 2007. 🙁<br />Jim goes through the manuscript notes that Adam had sent him.... and tells a story of when Adam went to Paris....( introducing other characters: Helene and Cécile). <br /><br />In section four: <br />Adam’s sister, Gwyn told Jim that many of the stories that Adam wrote were actually made up. <br />This section was a trip! <br /><br />The themes include sexual lust, incest, rage, rebellion, and a hopeful desire to rectify integrity by making amends. <br />It’s very thought- provoking ...thrilling...<br />compelling...<br />And the title, “Invisible” really fits....<br />referring to relationships, emotions, .... with a book within a book...<br />Addicting... page turning!!<br /><br />And...<br />SO THANKFUL ...<br />I’ve wasted hours on books ... that I just kept tossing ... and tossing... and tossing!!!<br /><br />I’m excited that there are still many more Paul Auster books for me to read ( I just wish the library carried them all)!</span>
  <a data-text-id="12416048574796307105" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/155683374?book_show_action=true">Mar 21, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shovelmonkey1" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shovelmonkey1" href="/user/show/3926982-shovelmonkey1">Shovelmonkey1</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571249527" href="/book/show/7513508-invisible">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">People who liked LOST and therefore don't expect an ending</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Shovelmonkey1 by: </span>
      1001 books list and all Austers previous work
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=bookcrossing-books">bookcrossing-books</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3926982-shovelmonkey1?shelf=1001-books">1001-books</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer155683374" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17297121929629259428">If you like to read a book with a nice story that makes sense and has a moral/point/definitive ending then you will not want to be friends with Paul Auster. Put the book down, that's it...gently..., now off you go and find something else to read.<br/><br/>If on the other hand you can't be dissuaded and carry on reading this the first thing to know is that you should probably disregard the blurb on the back - it only applies to the first 72 pages of the book. Maybe the person who wrote the blurb only got t</span>
  <span id="freeText17297121929629259428" style="display:none">If you like to read a book with a nice story that makes sense and has a moral/point/definitive ending then you will not want to be friends with Paul Auster. Put the book down, that's it...gently..., now off you go and find something else to read.<br /><br />If on the other hand you can't be dissuaded and carry on reading this the first thing to know is that you should probably disregard the blurb on the back - it only applies to the first 72 pages of the book. Maybe the person who wrote the blurb only got that far? Who knows. Now all of this probably sounds like it's gearing up to becoming the damning voice of someone who didn't like this book. Actually, I did like this book but again I'm never sure why I like Auster books. They have that appealing shiny-ness, that makes me go "oooh" then put my hands out to touch it even though I know I shouldn't,much like someone giving you a plate of mercury to play with. <br /><br />But behind the shiny is a matted world of weird, part of which, once again is loosely based on Austers own youth (wistful yearnings to be a writer, time in Paris translating poetry). How much of it is based on Austers on youth I don't know and can't be arsed to find out but right now I'm really hoping he doesn't have a sister. Although I'll say no more on that subject otherwise I'll start turning this review into one big spoiler. This book is really three stories tucked up together in between one cover and none of them really draw any satisfactory conclusions, except maybe that as you go through life you will meet a lot of people who are f*cked in the head and some of them might be French.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="17297121929629259428" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_262311939" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/262311939" />
    <a title="Blair" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/272909-blair"><img alt="Blair" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1493499323p2/272909.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/262311939?book_show_action=true">Jan 15, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Blair" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Blair" href="/user/show/272909-blair">Blair</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780571249510" href="/book/show/7783959-invisible">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=past-and-present">past-and-present</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/272909-blair?shelf=2000s-release">2000s-release</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer262311939" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7504518139624398384">This is what fiction should be, in my opinion. Absolutely dazzling, believable yet at times shocking, intellectual without being predictable or dry, compulsively readable but never inane, and above all, completely effortless.<br/><br/><i>Invisible</i> addresses three seasons in the life of a young man, Adam Walker. In 1967, Adam - a university student and wannabe poet - meets a French professor, Rudolf Born, at a party. What follows is a strange series of events culminating in two main outcomes: the first is Ada</span>
  <span id="freeText7504518139624398384" style="display:none">This is what fiction should be, in my opinion. Absolutely dazzling, believable yet at times shocking, intellectual without being predictable or dry, compulsively readable but never inane, and above all, completely effortless.<br /><br /><i>Invisible</i> addresses three seasons in the life of a young man, Adam Walker. In 1967, Adam - a university student and wannabe poet - meets a French professor, Rudolf Born, at a party. What follows is a strange series of events culminating in two main outcomes: the first is Adam's brief affair with Born's girlfriend, Margot, and the second is a random act of violence, committed by Born, which Adam is witness to. All of this takes place within the first seventy pages, with the rest of the book devoted to the effect of these events on Adam's life immediately afterwards, and their resonance in later years. All the usual themes of literary fiction are intact - memory, truth, loss, regret - but they aren't rendered in that self-consciously clever style I've grown so sick of over the past year. Predominantly, this is a story about obsession and sex: Adam is obsessed with exacting revenge on Born, as well as sexually obsessed with Margot, and later with another woman (whose identity I won't reveal here as it would give away a major - and very surprising - plot point).<br /><br />The perspective shifts: <i>Invisible</i> begins as a first-person account told by Adam himself, and at first, I expected the whole story to be told this way - but this being Auster, I should have known better than to expect a straightforward, linear narrative. In Part II, we jump forward to 2007, when Jim Freeman, a former acquaintance who hasn't spoken to Adam in almost forty years, receives a letter from his old friend with a partial manuscript - the first chapter of a possible book, titled 'Spring', which formed the first part of the story. The second chapter, 'Summer', is also told by Adam, but being far more personal and painful for him to recount, it is related in second person, placing the reader at the centre of an intimate and somewhat disturbing tale. In Part III, with Adam's health deteriorating, Jim is compelled to assemble chapter three, 'Fall', from a series of brief notes, and finally, he seeks out some of the others involved in the story to satisfy his own curiosity about both Adam's honesty and the after-effects of the events he has described. I want to say this is where things <i>really</i> get interesting - as the resulting accounts cast doubt on the truth of both Adam's story and Jim's interpretation - but that would make it sound like the book isn't riveting from the very first page, and it really is. <br /><br />I know Auster is an acquired taste - some of my Goodreads friends have given this book lukewarm reviews, and if you want a nice neat ending where everything's resolved and the truth is made clear, you won't find it here. But I am someone who generally likes the author's work, and this was by far the best of the Auster books I've read so far. I found it enormously compelling, beautifully written (it flows like a dream) and genuinely discomfiting. It lacks the obvious avant-garde tone of (for example) <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/431.The_New_York_Trilogy" title="The New York Trilogy by Paul Auster" rel="nofollow noopener">The New York Trilogy</a>, but retains a certain sense of oddness that's enough to give it a sharp edge. Altogether wonderful.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7504518139624398384" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer2517224671" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer3410885122989520906">A reader writes a book and a writer reads this book… That is what <i>Invisible</i> approximately comes up to on the side of the plot…<br/><blockquote>Put something in the wrong place, and even though it is still there – quite possibly smack under your nose – it can vanish for the rest of time.</blockquote><br/><i>Invisible</i> is a novel of moral anxiety, of moral qualms and of moral ambiguity. Who is really a villain and who is really a do-gooder? How much of the surrounding world remains invisible to us? How much of the other people’s life i</span>
  <span id="freeText3410885122989520906" style="display:none">A reader writes a book and a writer reads this book… That is what <i>Invisible</i> approximately comes up to on the side of the plot…<br /><blockquote>Put something in the wrong place, and even though it is still there – quite possibly smack under your nose – it can vanish for the rest of time.</blockquote><br /><i>Invisible</i> is a novel of moral anxiety, of moral qualms and of moral ambiguity. Who is really a villain and who is really a do-gooder? How much of the surrounding world remains invisible to us? How much of the other people’s life is invisible too?<br />And in the end the novel turns into a profound and ironical contemplation on the nature of truth and deception.<br />“And I gave my heart to know wisdom, and to know madness and folly: I perceived that this also is vexation of spirit.” <i>Ecclesiastes 1:17</i><br />How much of the world we see is truth and how much of it is lie?</span>

<div id="review_1085012558" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1085012558" />
    <a title="Fabian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian"><img alt="Fabian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1514061293p2/1036893.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1085012558?book_show_action=true">Oct 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Fabian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Fabian" href="/user/show/1036893-fabian">Fabian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1085012558" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5922607794774023575">My favorite Paul Auster novel so far. But if you've read "Oracle Night", "New York Trilogy", "Book of Illusions", then you've totally read "Invisible." The brand is unique*, but the plotless-ness can become quite disconcerting. I THOROUGHLY dug this one, reading it all in a day.<br/><br/>* "Timbuktu" is the only one of his that's not "meta." Just really really sad.</span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_117452005" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/117452005" />
    <a title="Max" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3148680-max"><img alt="Max" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1267997153p2/3148680.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/117452005?book_show_action=true">Aug 18, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Max" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Max" href="/user/show/3148680-max">Max</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer117452005" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer16825849307689263282">This is by far the worst book I've read in 2010. I couldnt even finish it; the thought of having to read another 100 pages of drivel led me to thumb through the last pages, only to realize I wasn't missing anything.<br/>How an author that wrote great novels such as The Book of Illusions or Man in The Dark can produce a book that contains no believable characters, no real story and only superficial and empty phrases is a mystery to me. The main character is a spineless loser, whose greatest accomplish</span>
  <span id="freeText16825849307689263282" style="display:none">This is by far the worst book I've read in 2010. I couldnt even finish it; the thought of having to read another 100 pages of drivel led me to thumb through the last pages, only to realize I wasn't missing anything.<br />How an author that wrote great novels such as The Book of Illusions or Man in The Dark can produce a book that contains no believable characters, no real story and only superficial and empty phrases is a mystery to me. The main character is a spineless loser, whose greatest accomplishment is 3 weeks of sex with a lacklustre French woman that just got dumped. His moral compass is stuck on 'thou shall not kill', and because he's got no imagination or anything else to show for, his biggest accomplishment is deciding to ruin the killer's life by convincing the killer's fiancee that the killer is a bad man. What an awful revenge. I don't care if he succeeded - if he did, it would be utterly unbelievable.<br />To add insult to injury, we also have a former classmate, who never saw our spineless zero in 40 years, but suddenly becomes fantastically interested in him - so much that he even considers his sexual exploits with his sister perfectly normal. This classmate is famous for his literary work, however in this book he decides to write like a first grader writing love letters to Lady Gaga. If Auster tried to shock me, he failed. If he tried to bore me, he succeeded. Man, that second part of the book was like a 24hr B&amp;B marathon. <br />This book was so bad I even threw it away - I couldn't stand the thought of spoiling anyone else's brain with such useless writing.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="16825849307689263282" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2975942734?book_show_action=true">Sep 13, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Steven Godin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Steven Godin" href="/user/show/26706841-steven-godin">Steven Godin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=postmodern-fiction">postmodern-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/26706841-steven-godin?shelf=america-canada">america-canada</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2975942734" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4717826366374848448">What happened to the Auster of old? The one that nods towards Beckett and Kafka? The one that made him stand out from the crowd. Whilst Invisible was full of the typical Auster tropes, one being the oddly detached roaming male narrator from the novel's first third, I think not only has Auster got more sentimental with age, but far more mainstream as well. This novel could have been written by so many other American novelists. It started out really well though, and I didn't have a problem with th</span>
  <span id="freeText4717826366374848448" style="display:none">What happened to the Auster of old? The one that nods towards Beckett and Kafka? The one that made him stand out from the crowd. Whilst Invisible was full of the typical Auster tropes, one being the oddly detached roaming male narrator from the novel's first third, I think not only has Auster got more sentimental with age, but far more mainstream as well. This novel could have been written by so many other American novelists. It started out really well though, and I didn't have a problem with the multiple narrators, nor the fact the novel has stories within the main story, and it was, earlier on, a real intriguing page-turner, as a three-way relationship developed, but it just fell away for me in the last third, like he didn't know what direction to go in, plus it contains a sloppy ending. What's strange, is that only a year later in 2010 he would write 'Sunset Park', a novel I personally think is his best since 1992's 'Leviathan'. </span>
  <a data-text-id="4717826366374848448" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_548835089" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/548835089" />
    <a title="Allie Riley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17643421-allie-riley"><img alt="Allie Riley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1522840920p2/17643421.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/548835089?book_show_action=true">Feb 28, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Allie Riley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Allie Riley" href="/user/show/17643421-allie-riley">Allie Riley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer548835089" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer10621256346836542915">1967 was a pivotal year for Adam Walker, for it was then, when he was a twenty-year-old student in New York, that he met the enigmatic Rudolph Bern and his then partner Margot at a party. Following the party he is witness to a murder and the consequences of that ripple throughout the novel. Combined with incest and intrigue these events keep you gripped to the very end. Nothing is quite as it seems and, even now, I am not entirely sure of the exact truth. <br/><br/>With regard to style, there are, effecti</span>
  <span id="freeText10621256346836542915" style="display:none">1967 was a pivotal year for Adam Walker, for it was then, when he was a twenty-year-old student in New York, that he met the enigmatic Rudolph Bern and his then partner Margot at a party. Following the party he is witness to a murder and the consequences of that ripple throughout the novel. Combined with incest and intrigue these events keep you gripped to the very end. Nothing is quite as it seems and, even now, I am not entirely sure of the exact truth. <br /><br />With regard to style, there are, effectively, three narrators, part II is in the second person and inverted commas are not used (something which usually annoys me). The characters felt extremely life-like and were well drawn. Although I have not met anyone like these people (and, frankly in some cases, I sincerely hope I never do) they were eminently believable. His writing is superb - just enough description to imagine yourself there - and the book is very tightly plotted indeed.<br /><br />This was my first foray into Auster's work. It will by no means be my last. Recommended.</span>
  <a data-text-id="10621256346836542915" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_59672199" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/59672199" />
    <a title="Tosh" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/133661-tosh"><img alt="Tosh" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1538010402p2/133661.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/59672199?book_show_action=true">Jun 14, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tosh" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tosh" href="/user/show/133661-tosh">Tosh</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer59672199" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11441654962033494950">I think a lot of people have given up on Auster, after his series of novels in the 21st Century. But "Invisible," his new one is a winner. He's a guy who keeps on working, no matter what, so you have to appreciate his work habits - but to me as a reader and once fan, well.. his novels became a boring horror show.<br/><br/>And just as I was about to heap him to junk history, his new novel arrives and it's an incredible narrative ride. I think Auster's technique or secret is that he is very much a page-turn</span>
  <span id="freeText11441654962033494950" style="display:none">I think a lot of people have given up on Auster, after his series of novels in the 21st Century. But "Invisible," his new one is a winner. He's a guy who keeps on working, no matter what, so you have to appreciate his work habits - but to me as a reader and once fan, well.. his novels became a boring horror show.<br /><br />And just as I was about to heap him to junk history, his new novel arrives and it's an incredible narrative ride. I think Auster's technique or secret is that he is very much a page-turner type of author. Almost summer reading on the beach or airplane/terminal type of writer - but smarter and more ambitious. Two, he writes in a very intimate manner. Almost like pulling you away from a l loud party gathering and telling you a secret or some really great gossip. And with that, Auster is back on what he does best, entertains. Very sexy, intimate, and interesting characters are in this book. It also deals with what one thinks of the romantic life of a writer or being a writer. And while reading this novel, I was thinking 'wow, I missed him." <br /><br />So yeah a really good novel by Auster that matches up with his earlier work from the 80's. I think he got his inspiration or muse or whatever one calls it back, and it shows in "Invisible."</span>
  <a data-text-id="11441654962033494950" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169484819" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1474052909647374349">This book caught my interest from page 1. It tells the tale of Adam, who stumbles into a friendship with some very odd people, Margot, and her lover, Born. The tale told is one that is captivating and intriguing. There is some definite ambiguity, and the reader is left wondering how much of Adam's tale is fiction or reality. I would highly recommend this book. The only problem that could annoy a reader is the change from first to second to third person narrative, but I quickly adapted to this</span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Bookmarks Magazine" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1008236-bookmarks-magazine"><img alt="Bookmarks Magazine" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1233856382p2/1008236.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79691755?book_show_action=true">Dec 02, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bookmarks Magazine" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bookmarks Magazine" href="/user/show/1008236-bookmarks-magazine">Bookmarks Magazine</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1008236-bookmarks-magazine?shelf=jan-feb-2010">jan-feb-2010</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79691755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11958228909319199369"><i>Invisible</i> contains many of the hallmarks of Auster's trade: formal literary devices and stylistic high jinks, psychological depth, elegant prose, and the manipulation of information, voices, and stories. Told against the background of 40 years of history, with shame and colonial guilt ever present, <i>Invisible </i>feels "warmer and more human than the stuff he's famous for" (<i>San Francisco Chronicle</i>) as well as less contrived and more hopeful. Indeed, notes the <i>New York Times Book Review</i>, it's "a love </span>
  <span id="freeText11958228909319199369" style="display:none"><i>Invisible</i> contains many of the hallmarks of Auster's trade: formal literary devices and stylistic high jinks, psychological depth, elegant prose, and the manipulation of information, voices, and stories. Told against the background of 40 years of history, with shame and colonial guilt ever present, <i>Invisible </i>feels "warmer and more human than the stuff he's famous for" (<i>San Francisco Chronicle</i>) as well as less contrived and more hopeful. Indeed, notes the <i>New York Times Book Review</i>, it's "a love story, or a series of intertwined love stories," with Walker at the core. A few critics thought that Auster's technique overwhelms the story, and one thought the characters uninteresting. But most agreed that <i>Invisible </i>is Auster's finest—and perhaps most accessible—novel to date. This is an excerpt of a review published in <a href="http://www.bookmarksmagazine.com" rel="nofollow noopener">Bookmarks magazine</a>.</span>

<div id="review_195324697" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/195324697" />
    <a title="Sam Quixote" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote"><img alt="Sam Quixote" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1529183438p2/5759543.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/195324697?book_show_action=true">Aug 06, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sam Quixote" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sam Quixote" href="/user/show/5759543-sam-quixote">Sam Quixote</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer195324697" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3922855205397089295">My feelings are conflicted with this book. I didn't hate it. I got through the book in 4 days, not rushing, and found Auster's style of writing still as compelling and easy to read as ever. On the other hand the story, while readable and familiar, just wasn't as compelling as I think Auster must have thought it was and neither was the bad guy nearly as menacing or complex. <br/> <br/>Paul Auster is a literary writer and is a highly respected, bestselling writer of post modern stories. Yet despite this he </span>
  <span id="freeText3922855205397089295" style="display:none">My feelings are conflicted with this book. I didn't hate it. I got through the book in 4 days, not rushing, and found Auster's style of writing still as compelling and easy to read as ever. On the other hand the story, while readable and familiar, just wasn't as compelling as I think Auster must have thought it was and neither was the bad guy nearly as menacing or complex. <br /> <br />Paul Auster is a literary writer and is a highly respected, bestselling writer of post modern stories. Yet despite this he can write a book that reads as fluidly as a thriller mostly because he is a master of pacing and knows precisely how to draw the reader in and when to step up the action. He showcases various styles in the four parts the book is divided in - the first person, the second person, the third person narrative styles are all used and served to highlight how talented the man is as well as to keep the story fresh for the reader (events are rehashed throughout). <br /> <br />Yet the central point of the story - I won't give it away but it comes at the end of Part 1 - just seemed contrived. And if you think about the circumstances, it wasn't that shocking. It serves to show a different side to the bad guy who until then seemed to be a foppish poseur. <br /> <br />The Auster trademarks are here: the bookish hero (trying to be a poet, talking about obscure poets and writers, working in a library), the mysterious benefactor, a dead family member/friend, and a manuscript. Familiar ground then, and yet despite Auster having written similar stories in this fashion many times before I was still compelled to read on. <br /> <br />The story never really goes anywhere though. The bookish hero is haunted by the events at the end of Part 1, Part 2 reveals a sordid past to the hero, Part 3 continues the haunted hero's recounting of the events at the end of Part 1 and a showdown with the bad guy, Part 4 throws a few twists in and ends on a mysterious metaphor.<br /><br />It's frustrating because I can't go into much detail without ruining the story but the story never really feels as dramatic as it thinks it is, nor as powerful in it's examination of some of the complex relationships in the book. By the end I didn't care and the last 30 pages dragged a bit. The final twist merited a simple shrug from me as I wasn't concerned with any of the characters at that point. <br /> <br />As for the title "Invisible", well, book groups can talk about what it means, I'll hazard a guess and say it might describe the real lives we lead and the lives we wished we'd led - the real life and the invisible life. But again, I didn't think about it too much, this isn't that sort of book. <br /> <br />It's not Auster's best but if you're a big fan of his like me you'll read it and find things to like about it. Overall though it's quite underwhelming and strangely forgettable. Still, it's not his worst and is an entertaining enough read. Glad he left himself out of this one though. Or did he...</span>
  <a data-text-id="3922855205397089295" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_84612900" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84612900" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84612900?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Gladia" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Gladia" href="/user/show/2651723-gladia">Gladia</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84612900" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12729110515248032857">Spring:<br/>New York: Walker meets Born at a party and the professor, just a few days later and for no good reason, offers to give money to the young student to make a literary magazine. Their professional partnership soon dies when Born stabs to death a boy who's trying to rob him and Walker.<br/>The story is weak since the very beginning and lacks of imagination to explain most of the events. Walker is depicted as a reasonable character but Born, an opinionated on everything French professor who seems t</span>
  <span id="freeText12729110515248032857" style="display:none">Spring:<br />New York: Walker meets Born at a party and the professor, just a few days later and for no good reason, offers to give money to the young student to make a literary magazine. Their professional partnership soon dies when Born stabs to death a boy who's trying to rob him and Walker.<br />The story is weak since the very beginning and lacks of imagination to explain most of the events. Walker is depicted as a reasonable character but Born, an opinionated on everything French professor who seems to have connections with some secret governmental agency, is remarkably annoying.<br /><br />Summer:<br />Still New York: the incestuous relationship between Walker and his sister.<br />For sure that's the best part of the novel. Emotional, touching, full of pathos, truthful and free of prejudices of any kind. I was almost disappointed by the fact that, later in the novel, Gwyn, Walker's sister denies the truth of the affair, but that doesn't change the fact that 'summer' is the what gives power to the whole book and it's a shame it's so short. (To be noticed: no Born appearances in summer)<br /><br />Fall:<br />Paris: Walker enrolls in the student abroad program and moves to the French capital, shortly meets Born and their conflict, ceased for that summer, starts again, together with the fall down of the quality of the story. Walker tries to ruin Born's soon to happen marriage but gets kicked out of the country with a month (mysterious powerful forces connected to Born).<br /><br />Ending:<br />'Fall' wasn't good but the end is very bad. We read parts of the diary of the daughter of Born's supposedly future wife. That's the worst part of the novel and Born comes back to dominate the story.<br /><br />Overall this was a weak novel. 'Summer' is the part that makes the novel worth to be read, but the rest is disappointing. Auster is a story teller and 'Invisible' is marked all over with his typical style but the end result failed my expectations. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12729110515248032857" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_3428675747" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3428675747" />
    <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen"><img alt="Daniel Clausen" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603533755p2/957634.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3428675747?book_show_action=true">Jul 06, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Daniel Clausen" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Daniel Clausen" href="/user/show/957634-daniel-clausen">Daniel Clausen</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/957634-daniel-clausen?shelf=books-of-2020">books-of-2020</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3428675747" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17730731847125034521">There are so many things to admire in this book: the way different levels of narrative are woven together; the unique construction of plot elements and story; his mastery of details within scenes. There is a level of craftsmanship that is undeniable. <br/><br/>And yet...something about the book was off-putting. The first thing that comes to mind is my inability to connect with Adam Walker as a character. The early 60s is a time and place that has drawn me in romantically (Norwegian Wood) before. And yet, </span>
  <span id="freeText17730731847125034521" style="display:none">There are so many things to admire in this book: the way different levels of narrative are woven together; the unique construction of plot elements and story; his mastery of details within scenes. There is a level of craftsmanship that is undeniable. <br /><br />And yet...something about the book was off-putting. The first thing that comes to mind is my inability to connect with Adam Walker as a character. The early 60s is a time and place that has drawn me in romantically (Norwegian Wood) before. And yet, I was unable to sympathize with Adam. Borne was an interesting character, but in fiction and film I've been introduced to too many sociopaths to be taken in by this minor one.<br /><br />Finally, I never felt any urgency. I never felt like there was something at stake. Would Adam's story come to light? Would Borne's? Oddly, because of the long passage of years, there is a sense in which time makes the stakes of past adventures and misadventures seem silly. <br /><br />Algeria, 1968, Vietnam...<br /><br />By the time we travel four decades from the 1960s to the 2000s, we are left to wonder how much the truth really matters, whether punishment for Borne means anything. We are also left with a nagging feeling that Adam Walker's life in the years since his time with Borne might have actually been more interesting. How does a wounded poet grow into a social warrior?<br /><br />Thus, just like in life, we are left at the end to wonder what it's all about. Does it matter whether the stories fo the characters are made public either in fiction or nonfictional form? Even if the invisible characters made it through the layers of invisibility -- from not cared about, to written about, to published accounts but in fiction, to a truly honest telling of events -- even if the characters could make I through this gauntlet of anonymity to the other side, what would it all mean in the end? And would it matter? <br /><br />These are the lingering, complex questions of a well-woven book...but are they enough for me to read it again? Probably not. </span>
  <a data-text-id="17730731847125034521" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_189130386" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/189130386" />
    <a title="Larry H" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h"><img alt="Larry H" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1577826084p2/5872506.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/189130386?book_show_action=true">Jul 25, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Larry H" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Larry H" href="/user/show/5872506-larry-h">Larry H</a>
      </span>

          added it




        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer189130386" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13288525727463145716">Although I try to read a lot of different books by different authors, I certainly have a group of favorites. If I see a new book by one of these writers (if I haven't been tracking them on Amazon), I will immediately by it when I see it. Paul Auster is one of those. The minute I saw his new book, Invisible, I nearly leapt on it (I'm crazy like that) and, of course, bought it. <br/><br/><br/><br/>Invisible is a well-written, intriguing and odd book. It starts in 1967 at Columbia University. Adam Walker is a college </span>
  <span id="freeText13288525727463145716" style="display:none">Although I try to read a lot of different books by different authors, I certainly have a group of favorites. If I see a new book by one of these writers (if I haven't been tracking them on Amazon), I will immediately by it when I see it. Paul Auster is one of those. The minute I saw his new book, Invisible, I nearly leapt on it (I'm crazy like that) and, of course, bought it. <br /><br /><br /><br />Invisible is a well-written, intriguing and odd book. It starts in 1967 at Columbia University. Adam Walker is a college student dreaming of life as a poet when he encounters Rudolf Born and his girlfriend, Margot, at a party. Rudolf and Margot immediately intrigue young Adam with their worldliness, and the couple becomes somewhat intrigued by him as well. What happens shortly thereafter is a shocking act of violence that has ramifications for the rest of the book. <br /><br /><br /><br />And that's where everything gets a little bit hinky. The book is divided into four sections. The first is narrated by Adam himself, the second and third sections are narrated by a college friend of Adam's (through Adam's words) and the fourth is narrated by another character and almost feels tacked on. There are a lot of big issues in this book--murder, incest, voyeurism, emotional anguish--yet not a lot of it resonates. I loved the story Auster was telling even as I felt uncomfortable reading pieces of it, but ultimately I was left somewhat unfulfilled. I guess I'll hope his next one has a bit more for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="13288525727463145716" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_156269237" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/156269237" />
    <a title="Adam" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/718537-adam"><img alt="Adam" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1200528647p2/718537.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/156269237?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam" href="/user/show/718537-adam">Adam</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781427208057" href="/book/show/6265736-invisible">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/718537-adam?shelf=audiobooks-a-z">audiobooks-a-z</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer156269237" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5492309840815679042">Post Lisen Review: Describing this book is really difficult. It is very well written, the prose is engaging, the events described in it are incredible yet it is certainly not for everyone. It blurs lines between facts and fiction, it shifts from different narrators and points of view and sums up one single story but at times doesn't feel completely coherent. Yet that it doesn't feel completely coherent is actually more of a strength than a weakness in this case. The book can be very sexually exp</span>
  <span id="freeText5492309840815679042" style="display:none">Post Lisen Review: Describing this book is really difficult. It is very well written, the prose is engaging, the events described in it are incredible yet it is certainly not for everyone. It blurs lines between facts and fiction, it shifts from different narrators and points of view and sums up one single story but at times doesn't feel completely coherent. Yet that it doesn't feel completely coherent is actually more of a strength than a weakness in this case. The book can be very sexually explicit and violent in completely surprising ways but it maintains an impeccable literary quality.<br /><br />The book takes place in three parts. The first part deals with a meeting between two men that leads to a very memorable incident of violence that resonates throughout. I felt like this was the strongest part of the book and the easiest to listen to.<br /><br />The second part was much more difficult to take. It dealt with a relationship between a brother and sister that was, let's just say, not exactly normal by societal standards. I found myself cringing at the section but not upset at the author for putting it in. It was just something where you suddenly see the protagonist in an entirely, disturbingly to my mind, different way. Part of me was revolted by him but at the same time I wanted to know what would happen. I really found this part to be the hardest to listen to.<br /><br />The third part deals with what happens after the main character dies and specifically deals with the man involved in the violent incident from the first part. While this part was less disturbing than the second part it was a little less engaging. Still the writing was excellent.<br /><br />All in all I hope to find more of Paul Auster on the library shelves and since this is his fifteenth novel there is a good chance I will.<br /><br />Pre-Listen Guess: I have no idea what to expect from this. If it's not a romance or a horribly bad mystery it'll be worth at least two stars though.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5492309840815679042" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_96453520" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/96453520" />
    <a title="Matt" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/805459-matt"><img alt="Matt" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1295148815p2/805459.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/96453520?book_show_action=true">Mar 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Matt" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Matt" href="/user/show/805459-matt">Matt</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer96453520" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1030691617979186845">So......<br/>This is the first book I've ever read by Paul Auster, and I must say, I was very impressed by this thrilling, disturbing and kinda weird book. His grasp of the narrative was absolute throughout, no small feat considering the extreme meta-literary gymnastics he puts the reader through. I enjoyed the unfolding mysteries, the multiple (and often (or maybe not) unreliable) narrators, the jumps forwards and backwards in time, and the globe-spanning locales.<br/>I guess I only knocked one star off </span>
  <span id="freeText1030691617979186845" style="display:none">So......<br />This is the first book I've ever read by Paul Auster, and I must say, I was very impressed by this thrilling, disturbing and kinda weird book. His grasp of the narrative was absolute throughout, no small feat considering the extreme meta-literary gymnastics he puts the reader through. I enjoyed the unfolding mysteries, the multiple (and often (or maybe not) unreliable) narrators, the jumps forwards and backwards in time, and the globe-spanning locales.<br />I guess I only knocked one star off because of the unanswered questions, especially the big one: did Adam Walker really, um, do that stuff (readers know what I mean). I understand the whole unreliable narrator bit, but I guess it seems a little passe to me, or if not, then this book should be the final statement on unreliable storytellers.<br />It seems like Auster went to too much trouble setting up these characters and intense situations to just leave us hanging.<br />Still, I was engrossed the whole time, and I would highly recommend it to someone looking for a challenging, post-modern drama. </span>
  <a data-text-id="1030691617979186845" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_79332547" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/79332547" />
    <a title="Jim Coughenour" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/180643-jim-coughenour"><img alt="Jim Coughenour" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1535843128p2/180643.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/79332547?book_show_action=true">Nov 29, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jim Coughenour" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jim Coughenour" href="/user/show/180643-jim-coughenour">Jim Coughenour</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer79332547" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11736555975589486343">One rainy spring night back in 1987, I wandered into Guild Bookstore (Lincoln Avenue, Chicago, extinct) and was beguiled by a sexy set of hardbacks – <i>The New York Trilogy</i> by Paul Auster – an author I'd never heard of. Guild was known more for its selection of esoteric lectures by Noam Chomksy than for modernist fiction; this set, published by Sun & Moon Press, looked like something I'd expect to find at the Art Institute. The books had a cool stylish look (including the author photo); the prose </span>
  <span id="freeText11736555975589486343" style="display:none">One rainy spring night back in 1987, I wandered into Guild Bookstore (Lincoln Avenue, Chicago, extinct) and was beguiled by a sexy set of hardbacks – <i>The New York Trilogy</i> by Paul Auster – an author I'd never heard of. Guild was known more for its selection of esoteric lectures by Noam Chomksy than for modernist fiction; this set, published by Sun &amp; Moon Press, looked like something I'd expect to find at the Art Institute. The books had a cool stylish look (including the author photo); the prose was set in a smart Bodini. I bought them for their dust jackets alone – which for me captured the romance of my recent wide-eyed visits to Manhattan, with its Bright Lights, Big City/Liquid Sky appeal.<br /><br />So I was delighted when I discovered the stories were as aesthetically satisfying as desaturated cityscapes on their covers. The trilogy offered an artfully convoluted set of variations on the classic urban detective story, Dashiell Hammett filtered through Beckett (and I'm undoubtedly echoing a cliché here) – but back then this opus seemed just about perfect: pure style in the guise of existentially-tortured substance.<br /><br />Regrettably, the experience wasn't easy to duplicate. Over the years I'd pick up each Auster book as it appeared; few provided the same thrill. From the distance of 20-odd years I observe that I've found more pleasure in his autobiographical essays (and the peculiar mini-classic <i>The Red Notebook</i>) than in his novels. In recent years, I enjoyed <i>Oracle Night</i> and <i>The Book of Illusions</i>; I cheerfully succumbed to the crowd-pleasing <i>Brooklyn Follies</i>; but I found <i>Man in the Dark</i> and <i>Travels in the Scriptorium</i> impossible to finish. (The objective correlative for that last one is the smell of old man's urine.)<br /><br />A couple weeks ago I read Auster's newest – <i>Invisible</i> – and fortunately had finished it before I read the evisceration by James Woods in this week's New Yorker. Woods begins his review with a précis of a mock-Auster novel and it's cruelly exact. It may even be fair. But I doubt it will affect my proclivity for Auster, however abused it's become.<br /><br />As I read Invisible, I felt as if I was watching an old friend perform his usual set of magic tricks. The plot unfolds in a set of familiar games, elliptical exercises on first-, second- and third-person narration, found manuscripts and unnerving coincidences, served up with usual tenor of agonized introspection. The plot, such as it is, completely collapses by the end. But no matter. The book is entertaining, even in its clichés. Auster may not be the transgressive modernist he affects to be (if indeed he does) but I still relish the ominous play of character and coincidence, as predictable as a ghost story, best read before bed with a dram of single malt.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11736555975589486343" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_87666937" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/87666937" />
    <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie"><img alt="Lars Guthrie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1199816668p2/757602.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/87666937?book_show_action=true">Jan 31, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lars Guthrie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lars Guthrie" href="/user/show/757602-lars-guthrie">Lars Guthrie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer87666937" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11245381430612754197">I hesitated in rating this one, because it was so unsettling. Then, being unsettled can be valuable. It is also Auster, mannered and clever, fey and twee, all at once, with an idiosyncratic voice so ubiquitous that it's hard differentiating between the characters behind the multiple first-person narratives in 'Invisible.' But like the 'The New York Trilogy,' Auster somehow pulls the rabbit out of the hat. Artifice becomes effective trope; the trick turns into truth.<br/><br/>'Invisible' is an investigatio</span>
  <span id="freeText11245381430612754197" style="display:none">I hesitated in rating this one, because it was so unsettling. Then, being unsettled can be valuable. It is also Auster, mannered and clever, fey and twee, all at once, with an idiosyncratic voice so ubiquitous that it's hard differentiating between the characters behind the multiple first-person narratives in 'Invisible.' But like the 'The New York Trilogy,' Auster somehow pulls the rabbit out of the hat. Artifice becomes effective trope; the trick turns into truth.<br /><br />'Invisible' is an investigation into what lies beneath the surface, in the case the surface of a blandly handsome and promising Columbia student of literature in 1967 who disappears from, then reappears (nearly a half century later) in the life of a former classmate--then disappears again. Adam Walker (every name has a peculiar significance in this novel) is thus invisible on several levels. But as you might expect, when Auster peels away the layers of this onion, we're still not sure what is really there.<br /><br />One thing that is buried beneath the surface of Walker's life is a bit of shockingly transgressive behavior. But like Martin in Edward Albee's 'The Goat, or, Who Is Sylvia?' Walker attracted my sympathy as much as my disapprobation, and made me think below the surface about what makes us classify certain behaviors as transgressive. <br /><br />In 'The Goat,' it's buggering a horned mammal; in 'Invisible' it's brother boffing sister. As in 'The Goat,' the transgression is so out there and matter-of-fact in the same moment that it is comic. A component of humor, after all, is a certain amount of discomfort.<br /><br />Likewise, I found an element of comedy in Auster's portrayal of Walker's Kurtz (with parallels to both Conrad's and Brando's), the peculiarly and significantly named Rudolf Born. Surely Auster intended that we chuckle at his parting view of Born--a decaying and overweight megalomaniac living in an isolated wilderness, muttering to himself as he is attended to by the natives. A deliciously magnificent villain, if ever there was one. <br /><br />While 'Invisible' can be disquieting and affected, I don't mean to imply anything negative about its readability. In fact, it is written much more like a best seller than 'The New York Trilogy,' and, for Auster, is quite accessible. I devoured it quickly, and with gusto. But the state of cognitive dissonance it engendered has stuck with me, and kept me returning to its theme: what is invisible, what is beneath the surface. <br /><br /><br /><br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="11245381430612754197" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('BZf8CHHFzsuwbujxCLlLHzocxSkgFciMibsDFeMlNpQ/sZXaypsHoxdFXJD/TEHA7EMi4e6YRuvSxUWkLmbEDg==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('ELd6Y5vSpu58KwpqUwG8cGu8duB7fGVUatBctuYGE9oqkROxIIxvhtsAvguk9LavveORKLXx6zMxrhoHK0XhQA==')})
  }
